        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties of the rutile-type TiO2 and Nb-doped TiO2 were investigated for the first time as Na-ion battery anodes.  Ti1-xNbxO2 thick-film electrodes without a binder and a conductive additive were prepd. using a sol-gel method followed by a gas-deposition method.  The TiO2 electrode showed reversible reactions of Na insertion/extn. accompanied by expansion/contraction of the TiO2 lattice.  Among the Ti1-xNbxO2 electrodes with x = 0-0.18, the Ti0.94Nb0.06O2 electrode exhibited the best cycling performance, with a reversible capacity of 160 mA h g-1 at the 50th cycle.  As the Li-ion battery anode, this electrode also attained an excellent rate capability, with a capacity of 120 mA h g-1 even at the high c.d. of 16.75 A g-1 (50C).  The improvements in the performances are attributed to a 3 orders of magnitude higher electronic cond. of Ti0.94Nb0.06O2 compared to that of TiO2.  This offers the possibility of Nb-doped rutile TiO2 as a Na-ion battery anode as well as a Li-ion battery anode.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2012.11.055"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takami, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Inagaki, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tatebayashi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saruwatari, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Honda, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Egusa, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Power and Long-Life Lithium-Ion Batteries Using Lithium Titanium Oxide Anode for Automotive and Stationary Power Applications</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-power and long-life lithium-ion batteries using lithium titanium oxide anode for automotive and stationary power applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Takami, Norio; Inagaki, Hiroki; Tatebayashi, Yoshinao; Saruwatari, Hidesato; Honda, Keizoh; Egusa, Shun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion batteries with a combination of a lithium titanium oxide (LTO, Li4/3Ti5/3O4) anode and 4-V-class cathodes, namely, LiMn2O4 (LMO) and LiNixCoyMn1-x-yO2 (NCM) cathode, have been developed for automotive and stationary power applications.  The 3 Ah-class LTO/LMO cell for high-power applications had a high output power d. of 3600 W/kg for 10 s pulse.  High-rate full cycling tests at 10 C rate exhibited a high capacity retention of 95% after 30000 cycles.  The capacity retention after 15 years at 35°C in the calendar life tests is predicted to be 93%.  Such a high-power and long-life performance of LTO/LMO cell is suitable for not only hybrid elec. vehicle (HEV) but also idling-stop system (ISS) applications.  The 20 Ah-class LTO/NCM cell with an energy d. of 90 Wh k/g exhibited a power d. of 2200 W/kg.  The storage life for floating charge at 100% state of charge (SOC) and the full cycle-life at 3 C rate are predicted to be 10 years at 45°C and 10,000 cycles, resp.  It was demonstrated that the LTO/NCM cells have an excellent balance of high-power, high-energy, low-temp., and long-life performance for not only elec. vehicle (EV) but also plug-in hybrid elec. vehicle (PHEV) and stationary power applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1038/s41560-018-0107-2"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schmuch, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wagner, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hörpel, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Placke, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Performance and Cost of Materials for Lithium-Based Rechargeable Automotive Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Zero-strain insertion material of Li[Li1/3Ti5/3]O4 for rechargeable lithium cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ohzuku, Tsutomu; Ueda, Atsushi; Yamamoto, Norihiro</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Li[Li1/3Ti5/3]O4 having a defect spinel-framework structure (Fd‾3m; a = 8.36 Å) was prepd. and examd. in nonaq. lithium cells.  Li[Li1/3Ti5/3]O4 (white in color) was reduced to Li2[Li1/3Ti5/3]O4 (dark blue) at a voltage of 1.55 V and the reaction was highly reversible.  X-ray diffraction measurements indicated that the lattice dimension did not change during the reaction. The defect spinel Li1.33Ti1.67O4 and the fully lithiated Li2.33Ti1.67O4 with ordered rock-salt-type structure show nearly identical X-ray diffractions.  Due to similar lattice consts. of both phases and the low scattering factor of the lithium ions, the identification of the insertion mechanism using X-ray powder diffraction is possible only by using high angle X-ray scans of several samples inserted with different amts. of lithium.  Precise anal. of the obtained data supplies the evidence for the presence of two distinct phases which are mutually interconvertible upon lithium exchange.  This result is in good agreement with electrochem. data postulating a two-phase mechanism from the const. elec. potential found during cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2005.05.053"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ariyoshi, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamato, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ohzuku, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Zero-Strain Insertion Mechanism of Li[Li<sub>1/3</sub>Ti<sub>5/3</sub>]O<sub>4</sub> for Advanced Lithium-ion (Shuttlecock) Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sub-micrometric Li4-xNaxTi5O12 (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.2) spinel as anode material exhibiting high rate capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yi, Ting-Feng; Yang, Shuang-Yuan; Li, Xiao-Ya; Yao, Jin-Han; Zhu, Yan-Rong; Zhu, Rong-Sun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

                        cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The synergic effects of Na and K co-doping on the crystal structure and electrochemical properties of Li4Ti5O12 as anode material for lithium ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Zhongxiao; Sun, Limei; Yang, Wenyun; Yang, Jinbo; Han, Songbai; Chen, Dongfeng; Liu, Yuntao; Liu, Xiangfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Sciences</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Masson SAS</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of Na and K co-doping on the crystal structure and electrochem. properties of Li4Ti5O12 have been comprehensively investigated by means of X-ray diffraction (XRD), neutron diffraction (ND), scanning electron microscope (SEM) and galvanostatic charge-discharge tests.  Rietveld refinements of XRD and ND data indicate that the lattice parameters increase with the doping of Na and K, and Na or K prefers to take 8a site (tetrahedral site) in Li3.98K0.01Na0.01Ti5O12.  The lattice parameter and unit cell vol. of Li3.98Na0.01K0.01Ti5O12 are even larger than that of Li3.98Na0.02Ti5O12 and Li3.98K0.02Ti5O12 indicating a cooperative effect of Na and K co-doping on the enlargement of lattice parameters and unit cell vol.. The electrochem. property results demonstrate that Na and K co-doped Li3.98Na0.01K0.01Ti5O12 has a larger reversible capacity and rate capability as anode material for lithium ion battery compared to un-doped Li4Ti5O12, Na-doped Li3.98Na0.02Ti5O12 or K-doped Li3.98Ka0.02Ti5O12, which indicates the significant synergic effect of Na and K co-doping on the improvement of the electrochem. performances of Li4Ti5O12.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1039/c6ra16717a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na<sup>+</sup> and Zr<sup>4+</sup> Co-doped Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> as Anode Materials with Superior Electrochemical Performance for Lithium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">90455</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">90461</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Mechanism of the Na-substituted spinel phase generation in a Li4Ti5O12 electrode via sodium-ion battery cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kitta, Mitsunori; Kohyama, Masanori</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemistry (Tokyo, Japan)</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society of Japan</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Spinel lithium titanate (LTO; Li4Ti5O12) attracts much attention as a neg. electrode material for a sodium-ion battery (SIB), while large vol. changes in Na-insertion and extn. processes prevent practical applications of LTO-based electrodes.  It is desirable to form a Na-substituted LTO phase as (Na3)8a(LiTi5)16d(O12)32e, which is expected to show excellent performance in a SIB, due to a small vol. change from a Na-inserted phase, (Na6)16c(LiTi5)16d(O12)32e, analogous to a strain-free LTO electrode in a LIB.  In this work, we have discovered that such a Na-substituted phase is really formed via the discharge (Na-extn.) process from a Na-inserted LTO electrode consisting of two phases as (Na6)16c(LiTi5)16d(O12)32e and (Li6)16c(LiTi5)16d(O12)32e.  The Na-substituted phase generation occurs by the discharge with a high c.d. about 10 C rate, which induces high electrochem. polarization, exceeding the Li-extn. electrochem. potential in the discharging cell voltage.  Thus both the Na-substituted LTO and pure LTO phases are formed due to the extn. of both Na+ and Li+ ions as an electrochem. equil. process.  The present finding is a significant step toward practical application of the LTO-based electrode in a SIB.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.9b00541"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kitta, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kataoka, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tanaka, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takeichi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kohyama, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Spinel-Type Sodium Titanium Oxide: A Promising Sodium-Insertion Material of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Spinel-type Li4Ti5O12 (LTO) is expected as a safe and stable neg. electrode material of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  However, its small lattice vol. is not sufficient as the Na+ intercalation host.  Alternatively, a Na-substituted spinel titanium oxide phase (Na3LiTi5O12, NTO) with a large lattice vol. should be promising.  In the previous work, it was discovered that such a NTO phase is partially generated in each LTO electrode particle during specific SIB cycling.  In the present report, the basic properties are examd. of this NTO phase in the NTO/LTO composite electrode.  The in-situ XRD measurement of the Na-insertion process revealed that smooth Na+ insertion preferentially occurs in the NTO region in the composite electrode because of small lattice expansion in the formation of the Na-inserted NTO phase as well as the smooth Na diffusion in the NTO phase.  Thus, the composite electrode contg. the NTO phase showed superior rate properties of Na insertion compared to the pristine LTO electrode and showed excellent cycle performance with high efficiency.  In addn., the unique stability of the spinel-type NTO phase was examd. by the d. functional theory calcn., and the effect of the mixed transition-metal (TM)-Li-O network at 16d and 32e sites in lithium-TM oxides was discussed so as to keep the spinel structure for the substitution of 8a-site Li ions by other cationic ions.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1088/1674-1056/21/2/028201"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.-Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Spinel Lithium Titanate (Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>) as Novel Anode Material for Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Battery</span>. <i>Chin.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries attract increasing attention for large-scale energy storage applications in renewable energy and smart grid.  However, the development of suitable anode materials remains a challenging issue.  Here we demonstrate that the spinel Li4Ti5O12, well-known as a 'zero-strain' anode for lithium-ion batteries, can also store sodium, displaying an average storage voltage of 0.91 V.  With an appropriate binder, the Li4Ti5O12 electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 155 mAh g(-1) and presents the best cyclability among all reported oxide-based anode materials.  Density functional theory calculations predict a three-phase separation mechanism, 2Li4Ti5O12+6Na(+)+6e(-)↔Li7Ti5O12+Na6LiTi5O12, which has been confirmed through in situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction and advanced scanning transmission electron microscope imaging techniques.  The three-phase separation reaction has never been seen in any insertion electrode materials for lithium- or sodium-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Spinel lithium titanate (LTO; Li4Ti5O12) is one of the promising materials for neg. electrodes of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  The stable charge-discharge performance of SIB cells using LTO electrodes depends on the reversible Na insertion-extn. mechanism of LTO, where the spinel lattice is expanded with Na insertion, and two phases, Na-inserted LTO (Na-LTO) and Li-inserted LTO (Li-LTO) phases, are generated.  These phases are confirmed using X-ray diffraction (XRD), while the mechanism of the two-phase coexistence with different lattice vols. is yet unclear.  Here, we investigate the detailed morphol. of the coexisting Na-LTO and Li-LTO phases using in situ XRD measurements and high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy (TEM) observation.  Na-LTO (a = 8.74 Å) and Li-LTO (a = 8.36 Å) phases are confirmed in both the electrochem. formed Na-inserted LTO electrode and the single-cryst. LTO thin specimen.  We obsd. that the Na-LTO/Li-LTO interface is parallel to the (001) plane, and contains an inevitable lattice mismatch along the interface, while the expansion of the Na-LTO phase can be partially relaxed normal to the interface.  We obsd. that the Na-LTO/Li-LTO interface has interface layers of lattice disordering with a 1-2 nm width, relaxing the lattice mismatch, as opposed to results from the previous scanning TEM observation.  How the different lattice vols. at the two-phase interface are relaxed should be the key issue in investigation of the mechanism of Na insertion and extn. in LTO electrodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref27" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5358"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barpanda, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oyama, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, S.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A 3.8-V Earth-Abundant Sodium Battery Electrode</span>. <i>Nat. (DLi+ ∼10-11 cm2 s-1), indicating a sluggish Na+ ion diffusion kinetics in Li4Ti5O12 comparing with that of Li+ ion.  Nanosizing the Li4Ti5O12 will be crit. to make it a suitable anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  The application of this novel in situ chem. sodiation method reported in this work provides a facile way and a new opportunity for in situ structure investigations of various sodium-ion battery materials and other systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1016/j.cplett.2019.136598"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tada, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kitta, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ozaki, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tanaka, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Comparative Study of Na<sub>3</sub>LiTi<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> and Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>: Geometric and Electronic Structures Obtained by Density Functional Theory Calculations</span>. <i>Chem. Phys. Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>731</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">136598</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cplett.2019.136598</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D731%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D136598%26journal%3DChem.%2BPhys.%2BLett.%26author%3DK.%2BTada%26author%3DM.%2BKitta%26author%3DH.%2BOzaki%26author%3DS.%2BTanaka%26title%3DA%2BComparative%2BStudy%2Bof%2BNa3LiTi5O12%2Band%2BLi4Ti5O12%253A%2BGeometric%2Band%2BElectronic%2BStructures%2BObtained%2Bby%2BDensity%2BFunctional%2BTheory%2BCalculations%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.cplett.2019.136598&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b21925&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.cplett.2019.136598&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comparative study of Na3LiTi5O12 and Li4Ti5O12: Geometric and electronic structures obtained by density functional theory calculations</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tada, Kohei; Kitta, Mitsunori; Ozaki, Hiroyuki; Tanaka, Shingo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Physics Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">731</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">136598</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHPLBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2614</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To investigate the potential of the recently reported spinel-type Na3LiTi5O12 (NTO) phase as a battery anode, its geometric and electronic structures were clarified and compared with those of Li4Ti5O12 (LTO), which is a well-known oxide anode material for Li-ion batteries.  The lattice const. of NTO was calcd. to be larger than that of LTO, whereas the corresponding electronic structures were similar.  This result implies that similarly to LTO, NTO is well suited for use as an anode material and is expected to be a good candidate for the anodes of Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.03.022"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kitta, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Akita, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tanaka, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kohyama, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Characterization of Two Phase Distribution in Electrochemically-Lithiated Spinel Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> Secondary Particles by Electron Energy-Loss Spectroscopy</span>. <i>J. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>237</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">26</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">32</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.03.022</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D237%26publication_year%3D2013%26pages%3D26-32%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DM.%2BKitta%26author%3DT.%2BAkita%26author%3DS.%2BTanaka%26author%3DM.%2BKohyama%26title%3DCharacterization%2Bof%2BTwo%2BPhase%2BDistribution%2Bin%2BElectrochemically-Lithiated%2BSpinel%2BLi4Ti5O12%2BSecondary%2BParticles%2Bby%2BElectron%2BEnergy-Loss%2BSpectroscopy%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2013.03.022&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b21925&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2013.03.022&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Characterization of two phase distribution in electrochemically-lithiated spinel Li4Ti5O12 secondary particles by electron energy-loss spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kitta, Mitsunori; Akita, Tomoki; Tanaka, Shingo; Kohyama, Masanori</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">237</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">26-32</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Spinel lithium titanate, Li4Ti5O12 (LTO) is attracting much attention as an alternative material replacing carbon-based anodes in lithium ion batteries, due to its high rate properties as well as its durability and safety.  The coexistence of the two phases, spinel Li4Ti5O12 and rock salt Li7Ti5O12, during the charge-discharge reactions is the key issue for the understanding of reaction mechanism and the development of LTO electrodes with improved performance.  However, the two-phase distribution morphol. in real LTO electrodes has not yet been reported, due to the difficulty in identifying the Li-inserted Li7Ti5O12 phase with high resolns.  Thus we apply the scanning transmission electron microscopy with electron energy loss spectroscopy (STEM-EELS) using the latest equipment with high energy and spatial resolns.  We have successfully identified the presence of the Li7Ti5O12 phase in Li-inserted LTO secondary particles, by the EELS anal. of Li compn., Ti oxidn. state, and local configurations around oxygen atoms, compared to the Li4Ti5O12 phase.  We have also obtained the two-phase distribution image in 50% Li-inserted LTO secondary particles, where sep. presence of both the phases is clearly revealed.  This indicates the particle-by-particle reaction mechanism, where the rate detg. process exists in the boundary between primary particles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.6b02828"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schmidt, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wilkening, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Discriminating the Mobile Ions from the Immobile Ones in Li<sub>4+<i>x</i></sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>: <sup>6</sup>Li NMR Reveals the Main Li<sup>+</sup> Diffusion Pathway and Proposes a Refined Lithiation Mechanism</span>. <i>J. Phys. Chem. C</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>120</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">11372</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">11381</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acs.jpcc.6b02828</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D120%26publication_year%3D2016%26pages%3D11372-11381%26journal%3DJ.%2BPhys.%2BChem.%2BC%26author%3DW.%2BSchmidt%26author%3DM.%2BWilkening%26title%3DDiscriminating%2Bthe%2BMobile%2BIons%2Bfrom%2Bthe%2BImmobile%2BOnes%2Bin%2BLi4%252BxTi5O12%253A%2B6Li%2BNMR%2BReveals%2Bthe%2BMain%2BLi%252B%2BDiffusion%2BPathway%2Band%2BProposes%2Ba%2BRefined%2BLithiation%2BMechanism%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.jpcc.6b02828&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b21925&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.jpcc.6b02828&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Discriminating the Mobile Ions from the Immobile Ones in Li4+xTi5O12: 6Li NMR Reveals the Main Li+ Diffusion Pathway and Proposes a Refined Lithiation Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schmidt, Walter; Wilkening, Martin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">120</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">21</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11372-11381</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">One of the most widely known anode materials with excellent cycling performance in lithium-ion batteries is zero-strain lithium titanate Li4Ti5O12 (LTO).  This is based on the highly reversible insertion and deinsertion of Li ions varying the compn. of Li4+xTi5O12 from x = 0 to x = 3.  The facile incorporation of Li is tightly connected with bulk Li ion dynamics.  Although macroscopic electrochem. properties have been studied in great detail, bulk Li ion diffusion pathways have rarely been probed exptl. from an at. scale point of view.  Here, we evaluated 1D and 2D 6Li magic angle spinning (MAS) NMR spectra recorded as a function of x to make the local magnetic changes during Li insertion visible.  This enabled us to directly show that rapid Li exchange involves the 8a and 16c sites, forming a 3D network throughout the LTO spinel structure.  In contrast, considering the time scale set by our NMR expts., the Li ions residing on the mixed occupied octahedral positions 16d are quite immobile.  Under the MAS conditions applied, they definitely do not participate in rapid Li self-diffusion as evidenced by rather long site-specific longitudinal relaxation times as well as the absence of any NMR coalescence effects.  Based on the NMR spectra, we propose a refined Li insertion mechanism which includes the initial formation of a highly conducting quasi solid soln. characterized by rapid Li ion exchange.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188aba4bfe54b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

aria-labelledby="pane-m1" class="tab__pane tab__pane-figures active"><figure data-id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Abstract</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#_i1"><img class="rightTab-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0010.gif" alt="" id="rightTab-tgr1"></a><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0001.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr1" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. SR-XRD spectra of purified samples. The Lindeman glass capillary (φ = 0.5 mm) filled with about 2 mg of material powder was tested by λ = 0.7 Å of a photon energy X-ray source. The XRD measurement was performed at a beam line of BL192B2 in the SPring-8 radiation factory, exposed for 20 s for 4 cycles in each sample. (a) XRD profiles of each corresponding purification cycle for <i>n</i> = 0–9, respectively. XRD peaks assigned with the LTO phase are marked with (▼) in pristine LTO powder for <i>n</i> = 0. The LTO peaks gradually decrease, while the NTO peaks (●) increase with purification cycles, suggesting the conversion of LTO to NTO. The LTO phase almost disappeared at the ninth cycle, the single-phase NTO could be obtained accompanied by a remarkable 220 peak.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of pristine LTO (a) and purified NTO (b) powders, respectively. The LTO material was commercially available (LT112 purchased from Ishihara Sangyo Kaisha, Ltd.). No morphological changes were observed between these two samples in both low-mag and high-mag images. This purification procedure did not provide serious effect on the powder morphology. Each primary particle, 100–500 nm in size, was not different in the morphology and crystal shape between these two samples. Thus, the lattice transformation from LTO to NTO should simply proceed without material damage.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Results of the Rietveld refinement for the purified NTO material. An inserted photograph presents about 50 g of the purified material. The refinement profile data were plotted as red dots and fitting error was shown in green solid lines. The reliable factors were optimized as <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 9.747, <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> = 1.697, <i>R</i><sub>B</sub> = 4.942, and <i>R</i><sub>F</sub> = 3.283, suggesting well-fitting results. The peak position of LTO and NTO phases is shown in blue and red lines, LTO peaks almost disappeared in the profile. The NTO phase with <i>a</i> = 8.746 Å was more than 96% showing purified powder is almost a single-phase NTO material. Other details of the refined atomic coordination are summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/suppl_file/am9b21925_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Single particle analysis of pristine LTO and purified NTO materials by analytical STEM. (a) Ti, O, Na, and Li elemental mapping by STEM–EDS and STEM–EELS spectral imaging. The EDS data were calculated by the P/B ZAF procedure to present a quantification map of elemental distribution. The NTO particle clearly shows uniform Na distribution, while the LTO particle is not shown. The integral electron count map of Li K edge EELS of the LTO and NTO particle are shown with the same color scale, where the Li intensity is clearly decreasing in the NTO particle. (b) EDS spectrum of LTO (blue line) and NTO (red line), as normalized by the Ti K intensity. The Na K intensity is clearly observed only in the NTO particle. (c) Low-loss EEL spectra of LTO (blue line) and NTO (red line). The Li K edge is remarkably decreasing in NTO but slightly observed, as Li remained in the NTO particle. (d) ELNES of the Ti L edge for LTO (blue line) and NTO (red line). Doublet splitting of each L<sub>3</sub> and L<sub>2</sub> white line showed the characteristic of Ti<sup>4+</sup>. (e) O K edge ELNES of LTO and NTO, corresponding to the color of (d). Pre-energy peaks and main-energy peaks were numbered 1, 2, and 3 to 5, respectively. (f) Histogram of the O/Ti ratio calculated from the integral electron counts of O K and Ti L ELNES extracted from spectral imaging pixels of LTO and NTO particles. Solid lines show Gaussian fitting for each histogram.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0005.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Characterization of Li-site of the LTO and NTO phase by <sup>6</sup>Li solid-state NMR recorded at room temperature. The LTO spectra (blue line) show doublet peaks with different magnetic fields corresponding to the 8a-site (lower side) and the 16d-site (higher side), respectively. While the remaining Li in the NTO phase showed a single peak well corresponding to the 16d-site. The peak of NTO spectra is broadening because of the more fluctuation of the oxygen position in the NTO lattice than the LTO lattice.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig6"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0006.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr6" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. In situ XRD spectra for the single-phase NTO electrode with various Na-insertion depths. The Mo Kα radiation (λ<sub>1</sub> and λ<sub>2</sub> = 0.7093187 and 0.713609 Å) was used for this experiment. The current density of Na-insertion and extraction is set to 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, and XRD spectra were recorded at 0.008° step with 1° min<sup>–1</sup> of scan rate during each OCV condition. All the spectra normalized their intensities with Al-220 peak. (a) In situ XRD spectra corresponding to the various Na-insertion depths pointed in the Na-insertion and extraction voltage profile. (b) Comparison on the in situ XRD spectra and their corresponding simulation spectra between the Na-insertion and extraction state. The simulation model of Na in the 8a-site was refer to the Rietveld refinement results described as <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/suppl_file/am9b21925_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2b</a>, and another model was prepared by the simple alternation of the Na site coordination from 8a (0, 0, 0) to 16c (1/8, 1/8, 1/8) in the <i>Fd</i>3̅<i>m</i> space group with <i>a</i> = 8.827 Å. In the experimental profiles, peak intensity of 222 and 400 were increasing with Na-insertion, while 111, 311, and 511 were decreasing. This feature was well corresponding into the simulation profiles in assuming of the Na-site alternation from 8a to 16c.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig7"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0007.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr7" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Electrochemical properties of the single-phase NTO electrode. (a) Na-insertion and extraction voltage profiles extracted at specific cycle during 200 cycles. The current density is 5/C = 30 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for each Na-insertion and extraction. Single voltage plateau slightly inclined was completely reproduced by 200 cycles, indicating a superior stability of this material during SIBs cycle. Plots of the Na-insertion capacity and C. E.  vs  cycle of the odd number are shown in the inset. The black circle and green triangle shows capacity and C. E. during 200 cycle, respectively. (b) Voltage profiles under various Na-insertion current densities. The Na-extraction current density is fixed to C/16 in whole the cycles. The experiment repeated for five cycles in each Na-insertion current densities. The circle plot of Na-insertion capacity vs cycle numbers for various Na-insertion current density was shown in the inset. The capacity plots revert to almost initial state after high current density experiment, suggested the stability of electrode under various Na-insertion current density.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig8"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0008.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr8" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Typical electrochemical charge–discharge voltage profiles of LTO/Li (blue) and NTO/Na half-cells, as shown based on the NHE potential. Galvanostatic current density was set to 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for LTO/Li and 1.5 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for NTO/Na half-cells, respectively because of relatively slow reaction rate of NTO/Na hal-cell. LTO/Li half-cell had a clear flat voltage profile for 170 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> of Li-insertion and extraction capacity region, showed a simple two-phase transformation reaction mechanism. The NTO/Na half-cell gave inclined the voltage profile with the 150 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> capacity region, suggesting that Na-insertion and extraction reaction of NTO would proceed with a solid-solution-like mechanism. The average electrochemical potential (vs NHE) of each cell and there potential gap (Δ) are also shown in the figure.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig9"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/medium/am9b21925_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0009.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr9" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. Several charge–discharge voltage profiles of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/NTO full-cell during 100 cycles. The galvanostatic current density of the cell is set to 10 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for the NTO negative electrode and cycled with 1.0 to 4.0 V cut-off range. The electrochemical capacity ratio of the negative/positive electrode (N/P) is tested as 0.8, with regards to the specific capacity of NTO is 120 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> is 70 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Plots of the discharge capacity and C. E. vs odd number cycle is shown in the inset. The black circle and green triangle shows the capacity and C. E. of during 100 cycle, respectively. The average cell voltage of the charge–discharge cycle is also shown in the figure.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/asset/images/large/am9b21925_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21925&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></li><li id="pane-m2" aria-labelledby="pane-m2" class="tab__pane tab__pane-references"><div class="refs-header-label"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i30">References</h2></div></div></div><hr></div><p class="rightTab-references-count">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Si-C hybrid nanofibers with a core-shell structure of Si nanoparticles confined in porous carbon nanofibers was obtained through a single-nozzle electrospinning technique.  The as-obtained Si@PCNF exhibits excellent properties in terms of cycling performance and rate capabilities for application as an anode material for LIBs.  Furthermore, confinement of nanoparticles in porous carbon nanofibers by single-nozzle spinning constructs a favorable platform for designing and prepg. high-performance catalysts, sensors, and electrode materials for Li and Na-based batteries as well as supercapacitors.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.9b02048"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">New Prelithiated V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> Superstructure for Lithium-Ion Batteries with Long Cycle Life and High Power</span>. <i>ACS Energy Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">31</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">38</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">V pentoxide (V2O5) is an attractive high capacity cathode material for Li ion batteries but is limited by the poor structural stability. Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/c6cs00776g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: Present and Future</span>. <i>Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c6cc09378g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, H.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zuo, T.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Novel P<sub>2</sub>-Type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/6</sub>Mg<sub>1/6</sub>Ti<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as an Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties of the rutile-type TiO2 and Nb-doped TiO2 were investigated for the first time as Na-ion battery anodes.  Ti1-xNbxO2 thick-film electrodes without a binder and a conductive additive were prepd. using a sol-gel method followed by a gas-deposition method.  The TiO2 electrode showed reversible reactions of Na insertion/extn. accompanied by expansion/contraction of the TiO2 lattice.  Among the Ti1-xNbxO2 electrodes with x = 0-0.18, the Ti0.94Nb0.06O2 electrode exhibited the best cycling performance, with a reversible capacity of 160 mA h g-1 at the 50th cycle.  As the Li-ion battery anode, this electrode also attained an excellent rate capability, with a capacity of 120 mA h g-1 even at the high c.d. of 16.75 A g-1 (50C).  The improvements in the performances are attributed to a 3 orders of magnitude higher electronic cond. of Ti0.94Nb0.06O2 compared to that of TiO2.                         rightTab-cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-power and long-life lithium-ion batteries using lithium titanium oxide anode for automotive and stationary power applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Takami, Norio; Inagaki, Hiroki; Tatebayashi, Yoshinao; Saruwatari, Hidesato; Honda, Keizoh; Egusa, Shun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-ion batteries with a combination of a lithium titanium oxide (LTO, Li4/3Ti5/3O4) anode and 4-V-class cathodes, namely, LiMn2O4 (LMO) and LiNixCoyMn1-x-yO2 (NCM) cathode, have been developed for automotive and stationary power applications.  The 3 Ah-class LTO/LMO cell for high-power applications had a high output power d. of 3600 W/kg for 10 s pulse.  High-rate full cycling tests at 10 C rate exhibited a high capacity retention of 95% after 30000 cycles.  The capacity retention after 15 years at 35°C in the calendar life tests is predicted to be 93%.  Such a high-power and long-life performance of LTO/LMO cell is suitable for not only hybrid elec. vehicle (HEV) but also idling-stop system (ISS) applications.  The 20 Ah-class LTO/NCM cell with an energy d. of 90 Wh k/g exhibited a power d. of 2200 W/kg.  The storage life for floating charge at 100% state of charge (SOC) and the full cycle-life at 3 C rate are predicted to be 10 years at 45°C and 10,000 cycles, resp.                         rightTab-cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The synergic effects of Na and K co-doping on the crystal structure and electrochemical properties of Li4Ti5O12 as anode material for lithium ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Zhongxiao; Sun, Limei; Yang, Wenyun; Yang, Jinbo; Han, Songbai; Chen, Dongfeng; Liu, Yuntao; Liu, Xiangfeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Sciences</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The effects of Na and K co-doping on the crystal structure and electrochem. properties of Li4Ti5O12 have been comprehensively investigated by means of X-ray diffraction (XRD), neutron diffraction (ND), scanning electron microscope (SEM) and galvanostatic charge-discharge tests.  Rietveld refinements of XRD and ND data indicate that the lattice parameters increase with the doping of Na and K, and Na or K prefers to take 8a site (tetrahedral site) in Li3.98K0.01Na0.01Ti5O12.  The lattice parameter and unit cell vol. of Li3.98Na0.01K0.01Ti5O12 are even larger than that of Li3.98Na0.02Ti5O12 and Li3.98K0.02Ti5O12 indicating a cooperative effect of Na and K co-doping on the enlargement of lattice parameters and unit cell vol.. The electrochem. property results demonstrate that Na and K co-doped Li3.98Na0.01K0.01Ti5O12 has a larger reversible capacity and rate capability as anode material for lithium ion battery compared to un-doped Li4Ti5O12, Na-doped Li3.98Na0.02Ti5O12 or K-doped Li3.98Ka0.02Ti5O12, which indicates the significant synergic effect of Na and K co-doping on the improvement of the electrochem. performances of Li4Ti5O12.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1039/c6ra16717a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na<sup>+</sup> and Zr<sup>4+</sup> Co-doped Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> as Anode Materials with Superior Electrochemical Performance for Lithium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">90455</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">90461</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Spinel lithium titanate (LTO; Li4Ti5O12) attracts much attention as a neg. electrode material for a sodium-ion battery (SIB), while large vol. changes in Na-insertion and extn. processes prevent practical applications of LTO-based electrodes.  It is desirable to form a Na-substituted LTO phase as (Na3)8a(LiTi5)16d(O12)32e, which is expected to show excellent performance in a SIB, due to a small vol. change from a Na-inserted phase, (Na6)16c(LiTi5)16d(O12)32e, analogous to a strain-free LTO electrode in a LIB.  In this work, we have discovered that such a Na-substituted phase is really formed via the discharge (Na-extn.) process from a Na-inserted LTO electrode consisting of two phases as (Na6)16c(LiTi5)16d(O12)32e and (Li6)16c(LiTi5)16d(O12)32e.  The Na-substituted phase generation occurs by the discharge with a high c.d. about 10 C rate, which induces high electrochem. polarization, exceeding the Li-extn. electrochem. potential in the discharging cell voltage.  Thus both the Na-substituted LTO and pure LTO phases are formed due to the extn. of both Na+ and Li+ ions as an electrochem. equil. process.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries attract increasing attention for large-scale energy storage applications in renewable energy and smart grid.  However, the development of suitable anode materials remains a challenging issue.  Here we demonstrate that the spinel Li4Ti5O12, well-known as a 'zero-strain' anode for lithium-ion batteries, can also store sodium, displaying an average storage voltage of 0.91 V.  With an appropriate binder, the Li4Ti5O12 electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 155 mAh g(-1) and presents the best cyclability among all reported oxide-based anode materials. Here, we evaluated 1D and 2D 6Li magic angle spinning (MAS) NMR spectra recorded as a function of x to make the local magnetic changes during Li insertion visible.  This enabled us to directly show that rapid Li exchange involves the 8a and 16c sites, forming a 3D network throughout the LTO spinel structure.  In contrast, considering the time scale set by our NMR expts., the Li ions residing on the mixed occupied octahedral positions 16d are quite immobile.  Under the MAS conditions applied, they definitely do not participate in rapid Li self-diffusion as evidenced by rather long site-specific longitudinal relaxation times as well as the absence of any NMR coalescence effects.  Based on the NMR spectra, we propose a refined Li insertion mechanism which includes the initial formation of a highly conducting quasi solid soln. characterized by rapid Li ion exchange.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i27">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b21925?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b21925</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed explanation of water oxidation reaction, detailed parameters of the XRD profile with purification cycle, TEM images of LTO and NTO particles, SR-XRD spectra of LTO and NTO, Detailed results of XRD Rietveld analysis, theoretical characterization of oxygen displacement, detailed parameters of in situ XRD results, EIS measurements during initial NTO/Na half-cell cycles, long-term cycle performance of NTO/Na half-cell, Na-insertion C-rate properties of LTO and NTO in Na-ion half-cells, GITT measurement, d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots of the voltage profiles of LTO/Li and NTO/Na half-cell, charge–discharge voltage profile of Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> positive electrode in Na-ion half-cell, photograph of dismantled full-cell after 100 cycles, and charge–discharge voltage profiles of full-cell with various current densities (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/suppl_file/am9b21925_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21925/suppl_file/am9b21925_si_001.pdf">am9b21925_si_001.pdf (6.38 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="93188aba4bfe54b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="93188aba4bfe54b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="93188aba4bfe54b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));

      };

        <script nonce="93188aba4bfe54b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="93188aba4bfe54b8-SJC">
$(document).ready(function(){
    var cookie = "";
    var uri = "/doi/full/10.1021/html_original_uri?id=123";
    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
        $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
        console.log("ACSPubs2 failed");
        //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
    });
});
